     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Tasmanian devil
      </b>
      (
      <i>
       Sarcophilus harrisii
      </i>
      ) is a
      <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">
       carnivorous
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">
       marsupial
      </a>
      of the family
      <a href="/wiki/Dasyuridae" title="Dasyuridae">
       Dasyuridae
      </a>
      , now found in the wild only on the
      <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
       Australian
      </a>
      island
      <a href="/wiki/States_and_territories_of_Australia" title="States and territories of Australia">
       state
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">
       Tasmania
      </a>
      . The size of a small
      <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">
       dog
      </a>
      , it became the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Largest_mammals#Marsupials_.28Marsupialia.29" title="Largest mammals">
       largest carnivorous marsupial
      </a>
      in the world following the
      <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">
       extinction
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">
       thylacine
      </a>
      in 1936. It is characterised by its stocky and muscular build, black fur, pungent odour, extremely loud and disturbing screech, keen sense of smell, and ferocity when feeding. The Tasmanian devil's large head and neck allow it to generate amongst the strongest bite per unit body mass of any extant mammal land predator,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and it hunts prey and scavenges
      <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">
       carrion
      </a>
      as well as eating household products if humans are living nearby. Although it usually is solitary, it sometimes eats with other devils and defecates in a communal location. Unlike most other dasyurids, the devil
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">
       thermoregulates
      </a>
      effectively and is active during the middle of the day without overheating. Despite its rotund appearance, the devil is capable of surprising speed and endurance, and can climb trees and swim across rivers.
     </p>
     <p>
      It is believed that ancient marsupials migrated from what is now South America to Australia tens of millions of years ago during the time of
      <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">
       Gondwana
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LAT-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inman_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Inman-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nilsson_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nilsson-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and that they evolved as Australia became more arid. Fossils of species similar to modern devils have been found, but it is not known whether they were ancestors of the contemporary species, or whether the current devils co-existed with these species. The date that the Tasmanian devil became
      <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">
       locally extinct
      </a>
      from the Australian mainland is unclear; most evidence suggests they had contracted to three
      <a href="/wiki/Relict" title="Relict">
       relict
      </a>
      populations around 3000 years ago. A tooth found in
      <a href="/wiki/Augusta,_Western_Australia" title="Augusta, Western Australia">
       Augusta, Western Australia
      </a>
      has been dated to 430 years ago, but archaeologist Oliver Brown disputes this and considers the devil's mainland extinction to have occurred around 3000 years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2006_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown2006-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This disappearance is usually blamed on
      <a href="/wiki/Dingo" title="Dingo">
       dingoes
      </a>
      , which are absent from Tasmania. Because they were seen as a threat to
      <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">
       livestock
      </a>
      and animals that humans hunted for fur in Tasmania, devils were hunted and became endangered. In 1941, the devils, which were originally seen as implacably vicious, became officially protected. Since then, scientists have contended that earlier concerns that the devils were the most significant threat to livestock were overestimated and misplaced.
     </p>
     <p>
      Devils are not monogamous, and their reproductive process is very robust and competitive. Males fight one another for the females, and then guard their partners to prevent female infidelity. Females can ovulate three times in as many weeks during the
      <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_breeder" title="Seasonal breeder">
       mating season
      </a>
      , and 80% of two-year-old females are seen to be pregnant during the annual mating season. Females average four breeding seasons in their life and give birth to 2030 live young after three weeks' gestation. The newborn are pink, lack fur, have indistinct facial features and weigh around 0.20g (0.0071oz) at birth. As there are only four nipples in the pouch, competition is fierce and few newborns survive. The young grow rapidly and are ejected from the pouch after around 100 days, weighing roughly 200g (7.1oz). The young become independent after around nine months, so the female spends most of her year in activities related to childbirth and rearing.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the late 1990s,
      <a href="/wiki/Devil_facial_tumour_disease" title="Devil facial tumour disease">
       devil facial tumour disease
      </a>
      has drastically reduced the devil population and now threatens the survival of the species, which in 2008 was declared to be
      <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">
       endangered
      </a>
      . Programs are currently being undertaken by the
      <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Tasmania" title="Government of Tasmania">
       Government of Tasmania
      </a>
      to reduce the impact of the disease, including an initiative to build up a group of healthy devils in captivity, isolated from the disease. While the
      <a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">
       thylacine
      </a>
      was extant it preyed on the devil, which targeted young and unattended thylacine cubs in their dens. Localised populations of devils have also been severely reduced by collisions with motor vehicles, particularly when they are eating
      <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">
       roadkill
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The devil is an iconic symbol of Tasmania and many organisations, groups and products associated with the state use the animal in their logos. It is seen as an important attractor of tourists to Tasmania and has come to worldwide attention through the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Looney_Tunes" title="Looney Tunes">
        Looney Tunes
       </a>
      </i>
      character
      <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Devil_(Looney_Tunes)" title="Tasmanian Devil (Looney Tunes)">
       of the same name
      </a>
      . Due to export restrictions and the failure of overseas devils to breed, there are almost no devils outside Australia except for any that have been illegally smuggled.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

        <ul>
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2">
          <a href="#Genetics">
           <span class="tocnumber">
            1.1
           </span>
           <span class="toctext">
            Genetics
           </span>
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3">
        <a href="#Description">

       </li>

     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">
       Taxonomy
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Believing it to be a type of
      <a href="/wiki/Opossum" title="Opossum">
       opossum
      </a>
      , naturalist
      <a href="/wiki/George_Prideaux_Robert_Harris" title="George Prideaux Robert Harris">
       George Harris
      </a>
      wrote the first published description of the Tasmanian devil in 1807, naming it
      <i>
       Didelphis ursina
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      due to its bearlike characteristics such as the round ear.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He had earlier made a presentation on the topic at the
      <a href="/wiki/Zoological_Society_of_London" title="Zoological Society of London">
       Zoological Society of London
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o8_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o8-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, that particular binomial name had been given to the
      <a href="/wiki/Common_wombat" title="Common wombat">
       common wombat
      </a>
      (later reclassified as
      <i>
       Vombatus ursinus
      </i>
      ) by
      <a href="/wiki/George_Shaw" title="George Shaw">
       George Shaw
      </a>
      in 1800, and was hence unavailable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MSW3-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1838 a specimen was named
      <i>
       Dasyurus laniarius
      </i>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">
       Richard Owen
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but by 1877 he had relegated it to
      <i>
       Sarcophilus
      </i>
      . The modern Tasmanian devil was named
      <i>
       Sarcophilus harrisii
      </i>
      ("Harris's meat-lover") by French naturalist
      <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Boitard" title="Pierre Boitard">
       Pierre Boitard
      </a>
      in 1841.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A later revision of the devil's taxonomy, published in 1987, attempted to change the species name to
      <i>
       Sarcophilus laniarius
      </i>
      based on mainland fossil records of only a few animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Werdelin1987_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Werdelin1987-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, this was not accepted by the taxonomic community at large; the name
      <i>
       S. harrisii
      </i>
      has been retained and
      <i>
       S. laniarius
      </i>
      relegated to a fossil species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MSW3-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "
      <a href="/wiki/Beelzebub" title="Beelzebub">
       Beelzebub
      </a>
      's pup" was an early vernacular name given to it by the explorers of Tasmania, in reference to a religious figure who is a prince of hell and an assistant of
      <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">
       Satan
      </a>
      ;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o8_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o8-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the explorers first encountered the animal by hearing its far-reaching vocalisations at night.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Related names that were used in the 19th century were
      <i>
       Sarcophilus satanicus
      </i>
      ("Satanic meatlover") and
      <i>
       Diabolus ursinus
      </i>
      ("ursine devil"), all due to early misconceptions of the devil as implacably vicious.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o8_9-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o8-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Tasmanian devil (
      <i>
       Sarcophilus harrisii
      </i>
      ) belongs to the family
      <a href="/wiki/Dasyuridae" title="Dasyuridae">
       Dasyuridae
      </a>
      . The genus
      <i>
       Sarcophilus
      </i>
      contains two other species, known only from
      <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">
       Pleistocene
      </a>
      fossils:
      <i>
       S. laniarius
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       S. moomaensis
      </i>
      . The relationships between the three species are not clear.
      <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">
       Phylogenetic
      </a>
      analysis shows that the devil is most closely related to
      <a href="/wiki/Quoll" title="Quoll">
       quolls
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krajewski1992_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Krajewski1992-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The roots of Australian marsupials are thought to trace back tens of millions of years to when much of the current southern hemisphere was part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">
       supercontinent
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">
       Gondwana
      </a>
      ; marsupials are believed to have originated in what is now South America and migrated across Antarctica, which had a temperate climate at the time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o33_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o33-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As soil degradation took hold, it is believed that the marsupials adapted to the more basic flora of Australia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o33_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o33-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to Pemberton, the possible ancestors of the devil may have needed to climb trees to acquire food, leading to a growth in size and the hopping gait of many marsupials. He speculated that these adaptations may have caused the contemporary devil's peculiar gait.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o34_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o34-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The specific lineage of the Tasmanian devil is theorised to have emerged during the
      <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">
       Miocene
      </a>
      , molecular evidence suggesting a split from the ancestors of quolls between 10 and 15 million years ago,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PwP16_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PwP16-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      when severe climate change came to bear in Australia, transforming the climate from warm and moist to an arid, dry ice age, resulting in mass extinctions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o34_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o34-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As most of their prey died of the cold, only a few carnivores survived, including the ancestors of the quoll and thylacine. It is speculated that the devil lineage may have arisen at this time to fill a niche in the ecosystem, as a scavenger that disposed of carrion left behind by the selective-eating thylacine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o34_17-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o34-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The extinct
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Glaucodon" title="Glaucodon">
        Glaucodon
       </a>
       ballaratensis
      </i>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">
       Pliocene
      </a>
      age has been dubbed an intermediate species between the quoll and devil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jenolan_Caves_Tasmanian_Devil_jaw.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Jenolan_Caves_Tasmanian_Devil_jaw.jpg/220px-Jenolan_Caves_Tasmanian_Devil_jaw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Jenolan_Caves_Tasmanian_Devil_jaw.jpg/330px-Jenolan_Caves_Tasmanian_Devil_jaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Jenolan_Caves_Tasmanian_Devil_jaw.jpg/440px-Jenolan_Caves_Tasmanian_Devil_jaw.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jenolan_Caves_Tasmanian_Devil_jaw.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A jawbone found in the mainland
        <a href="/wiki/Jenolan_Caves" title="Jenolan Caves">
         Jenolan Caves
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Fossil deposits in limestone caves at
      <a href="/wiki/Naracoorte,_South_Australia" title="Naracoorte, South Australia">
       Naracoorte, South Australia
      </a>
      , dating to the
      <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">
       Miocene
      </a>
      include specimens of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sarcophilus_laniarius" title="Sarcophilus laniarius">
        S. laniarius
       </a>
      </i>
      , which were around 15% larger and 50% heavier than modern devils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o35_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o35-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Older specimens believed to be 5070,000 years old were found in
      <a href="/wiki/Darling_Downs" title="Darling Downs">
       Darling Downs
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">
       Queensland
      </a>
      and in
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">
       Western Australia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o36_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o36-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is not clear whether the modern devil evolved from
      <i>
       S. laniarius
      </i>
      , or whether they coexisted at the time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o36_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o36-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Richard Owen argued for the latter hypothesis in the 19th century, based on fossils found in 1877 in
      <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">
       New South Wales
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o36_21-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o36-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Large bones attributed to
      <i>
       S. moornaensis
      </i>
      have been found in New South Wales,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o36_21-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-o36-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and it has been conjectured that these two extinct larger species may have hunted and scavenged.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o36_21-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-o36-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is known that there were several
      <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">
       genera
      </a>
      of thylacine millions of years ago, and that they ranged in size, the smaller being more reliant on foraging.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o37_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o37-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the devil and thylacine are similar, the extinction of the co-existing thylacine genera has been cited as evidence for an analogous history for the devils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o38_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o38-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been speculated that the smaller size of
      <i>
       S. laniarius
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       S. moornaensis
      </i>
      allowed them to adapt to the changing conditions more effectively and survive longer than the corresponding thylacines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o38_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o38-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the extinction of these two species came at a similar time to human habitation of Australia, hunting by humans and land clearance have been mooted as possible causes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o39_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o39-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Critics of this theory point out that as indigenous Australians only developed
      <a href="/wiki/Boomerang" title="Boomerang">
       boomerangs
      </a>
      and spears for hunting around 10,000 years ago, a critical fall in numbers due to systematic hunting is unlikely. They also point out that caves inhabited by Aborigines have a low proportion of bones and rock paintings of devils, and suggest that this is an indication that it was not a large part of indigenous lifestyle. A scientific report in 1910 claimed that Aborigines preferred the meat of herbivores rather than carnivores.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The other main theory for the extinction was that it was due to the climate change brought on by the most recent ice age.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o39_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o39-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      While dingoes are seen as the main reason for the disappearance of devils from the mainland, another theory is that the increasing aridity of the mainland caused it, while the population in Tasmania has been largely unaffected as the climate remains cool and moist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o40_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o40-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to this theory, the dingo was only a secondary cause.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o41_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o41-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As the devil is the thylacine's closest relative, there has been speculation that the
      <a href="/wiki/Thylacine#Modern_research_and_projects" title="Thylacine">
       thylacine could be revived
      </a>
      by combining DNA from museum samples of thylacines with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">
       ova
      </a>
      of the devil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">
       Genetics
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Tasmanian devil's
      <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">
       genome
      </a>
      was sequenced in 2010 by the
      <a href="/wiki/Wellcome_Trust_Sanger_Institute" title="Wellcome Trust Sanger Institute">
       Wellcome Trust Sanger Institute
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Like all dasyurids, the devil has 14 chromosomes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t143_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-t143-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devils have a low
      <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">
       genetic diversity
      </a>
      compared to other Australian marsupials and placental carnivores; this is consistent with a
      <a href="/wiki/Founder_effect" title="Founder effect">
       founder effect
      </a>
      as allelic size ranges were low and nearly continuous throughout all subpopulations measured. Allelic diversity was measured at 2.73.3 in the subpopulations sampled, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterozygosity" title="Heterozygosity">
       heterozygosity
      </a>
      was in the range 0.3860.467.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mj04_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mj04-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to a study by Menna Jones, "
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">
       gene flow
      </a>
      appears extensive up to 50km (31mi)", meaning a high assignment rate to source or close neighbour populations "in agreement with movement data. At larger scales (150250km or 90200mi), gene flow is reduced but there is no evidence for isolation by distance".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mj04_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-mj04-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Foster%27s_rule" title="Foster's rule">
       Island effects
      </a>
      may also have contributed to their low genetic diversity. Periods of low population density may also have created moderate
      <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">
       population bottlenecks
      </a>
      , reducing genetic diversity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mj04_31-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-mj04-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Low genetic diversity is thought to have been a feature in the Tasmanian devil population since the mid-
      <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">
       Holocene
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morris.26austin2012_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-morris.26austin2012-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Outbreaks of
      <a href="/wiki/Devil_facial_tumour_disease" title="Devil facial tumour disease">
       devil facial tumour disease
      </a>
      (DFTD) cause an increase in
      <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding" title="Inbreeding">
       inbreeding
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A sub-population of devils in the north-west of the state is genetically distinct from other devils,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but there is some exchange between the two groups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-draft-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One strand conformation polymorphism analysis (OSCP) on the
      <a href="/wiki/Major_histocompatibility_complex" title="Major histocompatibility complex">
       major histocompatibility complex
      </a>
      (MHC)
      <a href="/wiki/MHC_class_I" title="MHC class I">
       class I
      </a>
      domain taken from various locations across Tasmania showed 25 different types, and showed a different pattern of MHC types in north-western Tasmania to eastern Tasmania. Those devils in the east of the state have less MHC diversity; 30% are of the same type as the tumour (type 1), and 24% are of type A.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sid_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sid-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Seven of every ten devils in the east are of type A, D, G or 1, which are linked to DFTD; whereas only 55% of the western devils fall into these MHC categories. Of the 25 MHC types, 40% are exclusive to the western devils. Although the north-west population is less genetically diverse overall, it has higher MHC gene diversity, which allows them to mount an immune response to DFTD. According to this research, mixing the devils may increase the chance of disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sid_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-sid-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Of the fifteen different regions in Tasmania surveyed in this research, six were in the eastern half of the island. In the eastern half, Epping Forest had only two different types, 75% being type O. In the Buckland-Nugent area, only three types were present, and there were an average of 5.33 different types per location. In contrast, in the west, Cape Sorell yielded three types, and Togari North-Christmas Hills yielded six, but the other seven sites all had at least eight MHC types, and West Pencil Pine had 15 types. There was an average of 10.11 MHC types per site in the west.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sid_36-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-sid-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">
       Description
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_markings.jpg">
        <img alt="Two devils, sitting side by side, the one of left with a white stripe under its neck. They stand on a dirt patch. Stones can be seen in the background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1296" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tasmanian_Devil_markings.jpg/220px-Tasmanian_Devil_markings.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tasmanian_Devil_markings.jpg/330px-Tasmanian_Devil_markings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Tasmanian_Devil_markings.jpg/440px-Tasmanian_Devil_markings.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_markings.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Two devils, one without any white markings. Around 16% of wild devils have no markings.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TasmanianDevil_1888.jpg">
        <img alt="A Tasmanian devil with a horizontal white stripe below its neck is sitting on some rocks and pointing its neck 45 degrees above horizontal." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1365" data-file-width="2048" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/TasmanianDevil_1888.jpg/220px-TasmanianDevil_1888.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/TasmanianDevil_1888.jpg/330px-TasmanianDevil_1888.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/TasmanianDevil_1888.jpg/440px-TasmanianDevil_1888.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TasmanianDevil_1888.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Tasmanian devil's whiskers help it to locate prey in the dark.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animaldentition_sarcophilusharrisii.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="495" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Animaldentition_sarcophilusharrisii.png/220px-Animaldentition_sarcophilusharrisii.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Animaldentition_sarcophilusharrisii.png/330px-Animaldentition_sarcophilusharrisii.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Animaldentition_sarcophilusharrisii.png/440px-Animaldentition_sarcophilusharrisii.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animaldentition_sarcophilusharrisii.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Dentition, as illustrated in Knight's
        <i>
         Sketches in Natural History
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Tasmanian devil is the largest surviving carnivorous
      <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">
       marsupial
      </a>
      . It has a squat, thick build, with a large head and a tail which is about half its body length. Unusually for a marsupial, its forelegs are slightly longer than its hind legs, and devils can run up to 13km/h (8.1mph) for short distances. The fur is usually black, often with irregular white patches on the chest and rump (although approximately 16% of wild devils do not have white patches).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1983_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1983-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oandp4647_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-oandp4647-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These markings suggest that the devil is most active at dawn and dusk, and they are thought to draw biting attacks toward less important areas of the body, as fighting between devils often leads to a concentration of scars in that region.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oandp4647_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-oandp4647-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Males are usually larger than females, having an average head and body length of 652mm (25.7in), a 258mm (10.2in) tail and an average weight of 8kg (18lb). Females have an average head and body length of 570mm (22in), a 244mm (9.6in) tail and an average weight of 6kg (13lb),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1983_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1983-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although devils in western Tasmania tend to be smaller.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o118_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o118-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devils have five long toes on their forefeet, four pointing to the front and one coming out from the side, which gives the devil the ability to hold food. The hind feet have four toes, and the devils have non-retractable claws.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-draft-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The stocky devils have a relatively low
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_of_mass" title="Centre of mass">
       centre of mass
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o52_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o52-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Devils are fully grown at two years of age,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and few devils live longer than five years in the wild.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2008_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jones2008-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Possibly the longest-lived Tasmanian devil recorded was Coolah, a male devil which lived in captivity for more than seven years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Born in January 1997 at the
      <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Zoo_and_Botanical_Garden" title="Cincinnati Zoo and Botanical Garden">
       Cincinnati Zoo
      </a>
      , Coolah died in May 2004 at the
      <a href="/wiki/Fort_Wayne_Children%27s_Zoo" title="Fort Wayne Children's Zoo">
       Fort Wayne Children's Zoo
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UPI2004_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UPI2004-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The devil stores body fat in its tail, and healthy devils have fat tails.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The tail is largely non-prehensile and is important to its physiology, social behaviour and locomotion. It acts as a counterbalance to aid stability when the devil is moving quickly.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o46_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o46-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An ano-genital scent gland at the base of its tail is used to mark the ground behind the animal with its strong, pungent scent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pemberton1993_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pemberton1993-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The male has external testes in a pouch-like structure formed by lateral ventrocrural folds of the abdomen, which partially hides and protects them. The testes are subovoid in shape and the mean dimensions of 30 testes of adult males was 3.17cm 2.57cm (1.25in 1.01in).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g64_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-g64-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The female's pouch opens backwards, and is present throughout its life, unlike some other dasyurids.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g64_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-g64-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_skull.jpg">
        <img alt="A skull suspended from the rear on a thin post, shown in profile, with the jaw open at around 45 degrees, showing large canines and a large bone joint for the jaw. More bones can be seen in the background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2397" data-file-width="2898" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Tasmanian_Devil_skull.jpg/220px-Tasmanian_Devil_skull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Tasmanian_Devil_skull.jpg/330px-Tasmanian_Devil_skull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Tasmanian_Devil_skull.jpg/440px-Tasmanian_Devil_skull.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_skull.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Tasmanian devil skull in
        <a href="/wiki/Zoological_Museum_of_the_Zoological_Institute_of_the_Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Zoological Museum of the Zoological Institute of the Russian Academy of Sciences">
         The Museum of Zoology
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Petersburg" title="St. Petersburg">
         St. Petersburg
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
         Russia
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_devil_skeleton.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Tasmanian_devil_skeleton.jpg/220px-Tasmanian_devil_skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Tasmanian_devil_skeleton.jpg/330px-Tasmanian_devil_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Tasmanian_devil_skeleton.jpg/440px-Tasmanian_devil_skeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_devil_skeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Tasmanian devil skeleton on display at the
        <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Osteology" title="Museum of Osteology">
         Museum of Osteology
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City,_Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma City, Oklahoma">
         Oklahoma City, Oklahoma
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Tasmanian devil has an exceptionally strong bite for its size, generating a force of over 553N (124lbf).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wroe2005_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wroe2005-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The jaw can open to 7580 degrees, allowing the devil to generate the large amount of power to tear meat and crush bones
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o46_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o46-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      sufficient force to allow it to bite through thick metal wire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o20_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o20-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The power of the
      <a href="/wiki/Jaw" title="Jaw">
       jaws
      </a>
      is in part due to its comparatively large head. The teeth and jaws of Tasmanian devils resemble those of
      <a href="/wiki/Hyena" title="Hyena">
       hyenas
      </a>
      , an example of
      <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">
       convergent evolution
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o64_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o64-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dasyurid teeth resemble those of primitive marsupials. Like all dasyurids, the devil has prominent canines and cheek teeth. It has three pairs of lower incisors and four pairs of upper incisors. These are located at the top of the front of the devil's mouth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Like dogs, it has 42 teeth, however, unlike dogs, their teeth are not replaced after birth but grow continuously throughout life at a slow rate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o64_51-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o64-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has a "highly carnivorous
      <a href="/wiki/Dentition" title="Dentition">
       dentition
      </a>
      and
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/trophic" title="wikt:trophic">
       trophic
      </a>
      adaptations for bone consumption".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o44_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o44-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The devil has long claws that allow it to dig burrows and seek subterranean food easily and grip prey or mates strongly.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o64_51-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o64-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The teeth and claw strength allow the devil to attack wombats up to 30kg (66lb) in weight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o44_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o44-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The large neck and forebody that give the devil its strength also cause this strength to be biased towards the front half of the body; the lopsided, awkward, shuffling gait of the devil is attributed to this.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o53_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o53-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The devil has long
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrissae" title="Vibrissae">
       whiskers
      </a>
      on its face and in clumps on the top of the head. These help the devil locate prey when foraging in the dark, and aid in detecting when other devils are close during feeding.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o64_51-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-o64-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The whiskers can extend from the tip of the chin to the rear of the jaw and can cover the span of its shoulder.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o64_51-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-o64-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hearing is its dominant sense, and it also has an excellent sense of smell, which has a range of 1 kilometre (0.6mi).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o64_51-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-o64-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The devil, unlike other marsupials, has a "well-dened, saddle-shaped
      <a href="/wiki/Ectotympanic" title="Ectotympanic">
       ectotympanic
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since devils hunt at night, their vision seems to be strongest in
      <a href="/wiki/Monochromacy" title="Monochromacy">
       black and white
      </a>
      . In these conditions they can detect moving objects readily, but have difficulty seeing stationary objects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">
       Distribution and habitat
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Devils are found in all habitats on the island of Tasmania, including the outskirts of urban areas, and are distributed throughout the Tasmanian mainland and on
      <a href="/wiki/Robbins_Island_(Tasmania)" title="Robbins Island (Tasmania)">
       Robbins Island
      </a>
      (which is connected to mainland Tasmania at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_tide" title="Low tide">
       low tide
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vul_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vul-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The north-western population is located west of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forth_River,_Australia" title="Forth River, Australia">
       Forth River
      </a>
      and as far south as
      <a href="/wiki/Macquarie_Harbour" title="Macquarie Harbour">
       Macquarie Heads
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucntd_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-iucntd-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Previously, they were present on
      <a href="/wiki/Bruny_Island" title="Bruny Island">
       Bruny Island
      </a>
      from the 19th century, but there have been no records of them after 1900,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucntd_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-iucntd-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and they were introduced to
      <a href="/wiki/Badger_Island" title="Badger Island">
       Badger Island
      </a>
      in the mid-1990s but are thought to have died out by 2005.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The "core habitat" of the devils is considered to be within the "
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Tasmania" title="Climate of Tasmania">
       low to moderate annual rainfall zone
      </a>
      of eastern and north-western Tasmania".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_35-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-draft-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tasmanian devils particularly like dry
      <a href="/wiki/Sclerophyll" title="Sclerophyll">
       sclerophyll
      </a>
      forests and coastal woodlands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer2001_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fischer2001-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although they are not found at the highest altitudes of Tasmania, and their
      <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">
       population density
      </a>
      is low in the
      <a href="/wiki/Gymnoschoenus_sphaerocephalus" title="Gymnoschoenus sphaerocephalus">
       button grass
      </a>
      plains in the
      <a href="/wiki/South_West_Wilderness" title="South West Wilderness">
       south-west of the state
      </a>
      , their population is high in dry or mixed sclerophyll forests and coastal heaths. Devils prefer open forest to tall forest, and dry rather than wet forests.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are also found near roads where roadkill is prevalent, although the devils themselves are often killed by vehicles while retrieving the carrion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vul_56-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-vul-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the Threatened Species Scientific Committee, their versatility means that habitat modification from destruction is not seen as a major threat to the species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vul_56-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-vul-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The devil is directly linked to the
      <i>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dasyurotaenia_robusta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dasyurotaenia robusta (page does not exist)">
        Dasyurotaenia robusta
       </a>
      </i>
      , a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tapeworm" title="Tapeworm">
       tapeworm
      </a>
      which is classified as Rare under the Tasmanian Threatened Species Protection Act 1995. This tapeworm is found only in devils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology_and_behaviour">
       Ecology and behaviour
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_resting.jpg">
        <img alt="A devil lying belly down on dry scrub grass and dead leaves. It has stretched its front legs out in front of its face." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1488" data-file-width="2240" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Tasmanian_Devil_resting.jpg/220px-Tasmanian_Devil_resting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Tasmanian_Devil_resting.jpg/330px-Tasmanian_Devil_resting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Tasmanian_Devil_resting.jpg/440px-Tasmanian_Devil_resting.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_resting.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Although Tasmanian devils are nocturnal, they like to rest in the sun.
        <a href="/wiki/Scar" title="Scar">
         Scarring
        </a>
        from fighting is visible next to this devil's left eye
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Tasmanian devil is a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nocturnal_animal" title="Nocturnal animal">
       nocturnal
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular" title="Crepuscular">
       crepuscular
      </a>
      hunter, spending the days in dense bush or in a hole.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer2001_57-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fischer2001-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been speculated that nocturnalism may have been adopted to avoid predation by eagles and humans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o129_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o129-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Young devils are predominantly crepuscular.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o69_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o69-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is no evidence of
      <a href="/wiki/Torpor" title="Torpor">
       torpor
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb98_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb98-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Young devils can climb trees, but this becomes more difficult as they grow larger.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ors_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ors-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devils can scale trees of trunk diameter larger than 40cm (16in), which tend to have no small side branches to hand onto, up to a height of around 2.53m (810ft). Devils that are yet to reach maturity can climb shrubs to a height of 4 metres (13.1ft), and can climb a tree to 7m (25ft) if it is not vertical.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb00_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb00-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Adult devils may eat young devils if they are very hungry, so this climbing behaviour may be an adaptation to allow young devils to escape.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devils can also swim and have been observed crossing rivers that are 50 metres (160ft) in width, including icy cold waterways, apparently enthusiastically.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ors_61-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ors-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tasmanian devils do not form packs, but rather spend most of their time alone once weaned.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer2001_57-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fischer2001-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o69_59-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o69-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Classically considered as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solitary_animals" title="Solitary animals">
       solitary animals
      </a>
      , their social interactions were poorly understood. However, a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_study" title="Field study">
       field study
      </a>
      published in 2009 shed some light on this. Tasmanian devils in
      <a href="/wiki/Narawntapu_National_Park" title="Narawntapu National Park">
       Narawntapu National Park
      </a>
      were fitted with proximity sensing
      <a href="/wiki/Tracking_collar" title="Tracking collar">
       radio collars
      </a>
      which recorded their interactions with other devils over several months from February to June 2006. This revealed that all devils were part of a single huge contact network, characterised by male-female interactions during mating season, while female-female interactions were the most common at other times, although frequency and patterns of contact did not vary markedly between seasons. Previously thought to fight over food, males only rarely interacted with other males.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hence, all devils in a region are part of a single social network.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are considered to be
      <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">
       non-territorial
      </a>
      in general, but females are territorial around their dens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This allows a higher total mass of devils to occupy a given area than territorial animals, without conflict.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oran_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-oran-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tasmanian devils instead occupy a
      <a href="/wiki/Home_range" title="Home range">
       home range
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a period of between two and four weeks, devils' home ranges are estimated to vary between 4 and 27km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      (990 and 6,670 acres), with an average of 13km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      (3,200 acres).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The location and geometry of these areas depend on the distribution of food, particularly wallabies and
      <a href="/wiki/Pademelon" title="Pademelon">
       pademelons
      </a>
      nearby.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oran_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-oran-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Devils use three or four dens regularly. Dens formerly owned by wombats are especially prized as maternity dens because of their security. Dense vegetation near creeks, thick grass tussocks, and caves are also used as dens. Adult devils use the same dens for life. It is believed that, as a secure den is highly prized, some may have been used for several centuries by generations of animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oran_67-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-oran-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies have suggested that food security is less important than den security, as habitat destruction that impacts the latter has had more effect on mortality rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oran_67-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-oran-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Young pups remain in one den with their mother, and other devils are mobile,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oran_67-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-oran-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      changing dens every 13 days and travelling a
      <a href="/wiki/Mean" title="Mean">
       mean
      </a>
      distance of 8.6 kilometres (5.3mi) every night.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, there are also reports that an upper bound can be 50 kilometres (31mi) per night. They choose to travel through lowlands, saddles and along the banks of creeks, particularly preferring carved-out tracks and livestock paths and eschewing steep slopes and rocky terrain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o118_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o118-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The amount of movement is believed to be similar throughout the year, except for mothers who have given birth recently.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The similarity in travel distances for males and females is unusual for sexually dimorphic, solitary carnivores. As a male needs more food, he will spend more time eating than travelling. Devils typically make circuits of their home range during their hunts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oran_67-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-oran-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In areas near human habitation, they are known to steal clothes, blankets and pillows and take them for use in dens in wooden buildings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      While the dasyurids have similar diet and anatomy, differing body sizes affect
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">
       thermoregulation
      </a>
      and thus behaviour.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t148_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-t148-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In ambient temperatures between 5 and 30C (41 and 86F), the devil was able to maintain a body temperature between 37.4 and 38C (99.3 and 100.4F). When the temperature was raised to 40C (104F), and the humidity to 50%, the devil's body temperature spiked upwards by 2C (3.6F) within 60 minutes, but then steadily decreased back to the starting temperature after a further two hours, and remained there for two more hours. During this time, the devil drank water and showed no visible signs of discomfort, leading scientists to believe that sweating and evaporative cooling is its primary means of heat dissipation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A later study found that devils pant but do not sweat to release heat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_35-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-draft-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast, many other marsupials were unable to keep their body temperatures down.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t149_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-t149-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the smaller animals have to live in hotter and more arid conditions to which they are less well-adapted, they take up a nocturnal lifestyle and drop their body temperatures during the day, whereas the devil is active in the day and its body temperature varies by 1.8C (3.2F) from its minimum at night to the maximum in the middle of the day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The standard
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolic_rate" title="Metabolic rate">
       metabolic rate
      </a>
      of a Tasmanian devil is 141 kJ/kg (15.3
      <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">
       kcal
      </a>
      /lb) per day, many times lower than smaller marsupials. A 5-kilogram (11lb) devil uses 712 kilojoules (170kcal) per day. The field metabolic rate is 407 kJ/kg (44.1 kcal/lb).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t149_73-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-t149-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Along with quolls, Tasmanian devils have a metabolic rate comparable to non-carnivorous marsupials of a similar size. This differs from placental carnivores, which have comparatively high basal metabolic rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A study of devils showed a loss of weight from 7.9 to 7.1 kilograms (17 to 16lb) from summer to winter, but in the same time, daily energy consumption increased from 2,591 to 2,890 kilojoules (619 to 691kcal). This is equivalent to an increase in food consumption from 518 to 578 grams (18.3 to 20.4oz).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t150_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-t150-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The diet is protein-based with 70% water content. For every 1 gram (0.035oz) of insects consumed, 3.5 kilojoules (0.84kcal) of energy are produced, while a corresponding amount of wallaby meat generated 5.0 kilojoules (1.2kcal).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t150_76-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-t150-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In terms of its body mass, the devil eats only a quarter of the
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_quoll" title="Eastern quoll">
       eastern quoll
      </a>
      's intake,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t150_76-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-t150-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      allowing it to survive longer during food shortages.
     </p>
     <p>
      The devil is a
      <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">
       keystone species
      </a>
      in the ecosystem of Tasmania.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STTDNSept10_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-STTDNSept10-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">
       Feeding
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Devil-eating-roadkill.jpg">
        <img alt="A black devil standing on a patch of cut grass in a paddock, next to a wire fence. It is biting into the torn carcass of an animal." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1006" data-file-width="1519" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Devil-eating-roadkill.jpg/220px-Devil-eating-roadkill.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Devil-eating-roadkill.jpg/330px-Devil-eating-roadkill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Devil-eating-roadkill.jpg/440px-Devil-eating-roadkill.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Devil-eating-roadkill.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A devil eating roadkill
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Tasmanian devils can take prey up to the size of a small
      <a href="/wiki/Kangaroo" title="Kangaroo">
       kangaroo
      </a>
      , but in practice they are opportunistic and eat
      <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">
       carrion
      </a>
      more often than they hunt live prey. Although the devil favours
      <a href="/wiki/Wombat" title="Wombat">
       wombats
      </a>
      because of the ease of predation and high fat content, it will eat all small native mammals such as
      <a href="/wiki/Bettong" title="Bettong">
       bettong
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Potoroo" title="Potoroo">
       potoroos
      </a>
      , domestic mammals (including sheep), birds, fish, fruit, vegetable matter, insects, tadpoles, frogs and reptiles. Their diet is widely varied and depends on the food available.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-of_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-of-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orit_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-orit-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o108_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o108-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before the extinction of the thylacine, the Tasmanian devil ate thylacine cubs left alone in dens when their parents were away. This may have helped to hasten the extinction of the thylacine, which also ate devils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o44_53-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o44-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are known to hunt water rats by the sea and forage on dead fish that have been washed ashore. Near human habitation, they can also steal shoes and chew on them,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-of_78-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-of-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and eat the legs of otherwise robust sheep when they have slipped in wooden shearing sheds, leaving their legs dangling below.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ors_61-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ors-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other unusual matter observed in devil scats includes collars and tags of devoured animals, intact echidna spines, pencil, plastic and jeans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o129_58-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o129-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although devils can bite through metal traps, they tend to reserve their strong jaws for escaping captivity rather than breaking into food storage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o129_58-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o129-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to their relative lack of speed, they can not run down a wallaby or a rabbit, but they can attack animals that have become slow due to illness.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-of_78-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-of-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They survey flocks of sheep by sniffing them from 1015m away and attack if the prey is ill. The sheep stamp their feet in a show of strength.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o129_58-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-o129-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
