         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite their lack of extreme speed, there have been reports that devils can run at 25km/h for 1.5km, and it has been conjectured that, before European immigration and the introduction of livestock, vehicles and
      <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">
       roadkill
      </a>
      , they would have had to chase other native animals at a reasonable pace to find food.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ors_61-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-ors-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pemberton has reported that they can average 10km/h for "extended periods" on several nights per week, and that they run for long distances before sitting still for up to half an hour, something that has been interpreted as evidence of ambush predation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ors_61-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-ors-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Devils can dig to forage corpses, in one case digging down to eat the corpse of a buried horse that had died due to illness. They are known to eat animal cadavers by first ripping out the digestive system, which is the softest part of the anatomy, and they often reside in the resulting cavity while they are eating.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-of_78-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-of-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On average, devils eat about 15% of their body weight each day, although they can eat up to 40% of their body weight in 30 minutes if the opportunity arises.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pemberton1993_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pemberton1993-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This means they can become very heavy and lethargic after a large meal; in this state they tend to waddle away slowly and lie down, becoming easy to approach. This has led to a belief that such eating habits became possible due to the lack of a predator to attack such bloated individuals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orit_79-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-orit-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tasmanian devils can eliminate all traces of a carcass of a smaller animal, devouring the bones and fur if desired.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinner_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dinner-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In this respect, devils have earned the gratitude of Tasmanian farmers, as the speed at which they clean a carcass helps prevent the spread of insects that might otherwise harm livestock.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o14_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o14-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some of these dead animals are disposed of when the devils haul off the excess feed back to their residence to continue eating at a later time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-of_78-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-of-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The diet of a devil can vary substantially for males and females, and seasonally, according to studies at Cradle Mountain. In winter, males prefer medium mammals over larger ones, with a ratio of 4:5, but in summer, they prefer larger prey in a 7:2 ratio. These two categories accounted for more than 95% of the diet. Females are less inclined to target large prey, but have the same seasonal bias. In winter, large and medium mammals account for 25% and 58% each, with 7% small mammals and 10% birds. In summer, the first two categories account for 61% and 37% respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb98_60-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb98-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Juvenile devils are sometimes known to climb trees;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in addition to small vertebrates and invertebrates, juveniles climb trees to eat grubs and birds' eggs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o69_59-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o69-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Juveniles have also been observed climbing into nests and capturing birds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb00_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb00-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Throughout the year, adult devils derive 16.2% of their biomass intake from arboreal species, almost all of which is possum meat, just 1.0% being large birds. From February to July, subadult devils derive 35.8% of their biomass intake from arboreal life, 12.2% being small birds and 23.2% being possums. Female devils in winter source 40.0% of their intake from arboreal species, including 26.7% from possums and 8.9% from various birds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb00_63-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb00-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Not all of these animals were caught while they were in trees, but this high figure for females, which is higher than for male spotted-tailed quolls during the same season, is unusual, as the devil has inferior tree climbing skills.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb00_63-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb00-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devils_feeding.jpg">
        <img alt="Three Tasmanian devils standing on bark chips huddled with their heads close together." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="1936" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tasmanian_Devils_feeding.jpg/220px-Tasmanian_Devils_feeding.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tasmanian_Devils_feeding.jpg/330px-Tasmanian_Devils_feeding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tasmanian_Devils_feeding.jpg/440px-Tasmanian_Devils_feeding.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devils_feeding.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Three Tasmanian devils feeding. Eating is a social event for the Tasmanian devil, and groups of 2 to 5 are common
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Although they hunt alone,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      there have been unsubstantiated claims of communal hunting, where one devil drives prey out of its habitat and an accomplice attacks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-of_78-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-of-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Eating is a social event for the Tasmanian devil. This combination of a solitary animal that eats communally makes the devil unique among carnivores.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o69_59-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-o69-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Much of the noise attributed to the animal is a result of raucous communal eating, at which up to 12 individuals can gather,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pemberton1993_46-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pemberton1993-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although groups of two to five are common;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      it can often be heard several kilometres away. This has been interpreted as notifications to colleagues to share in the meal, so that food is not wasted by rot and energy is saved.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-of_78-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-of-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The amount of noise is correlated to the size of the carcass.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-of_78-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-of-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The devils eat in accordance with a system. Juveniles are active at dusk, so they tend to reach the source before the adults.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orit_79-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-orit-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Typically, the
      <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(ethology)" title="Dominance (ethology)">
       dominant
      </a>
      animal eats until it is satiated and leaves, fighting off any challengers in the meantime. Defeated animals run into the bush with their hair and tail erect, their conqueror in pursuit and biting their victim's rear where possible. Disputes are less common as the food source increases as the motive appears to be getting sufficient food rather than oppressing other devils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orit_79-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-orit-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When quolls are eating a carcass, devils will tend to chase them away.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb00_63-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb00-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is a substantial problem for spotted-tailed quolls, as they kill relatively large possums and cannot finish their meal before devils arrive. In contrast, the smaller eastern quolls predate much smaller victims, and can complete feeding before devils turn up.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb00_63-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb00-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is seen as a possible reason for the relatively small population of spotted-tailed quolls.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb00_63-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-jb00-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A study of feeding devils identified twenty physical postures, including their characteristic vicious yawn, and eleven different vocal sounds that devils use to communicate as they feed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pemberton1993_46-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pemberton1993-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They usually establish dominance by sound and physical posturing,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinner_82-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dinner-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although fighting does occur.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pemberton1993_46-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pemberton1993-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The white patches on the devil are visible to the night-vision of its colleagues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orit_79-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-orit-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chemical gestures are also used.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orit_79-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-orit-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Adult males are the most aggressive,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and scarring is common.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They can also stand on their hind legs and push each other's shoulders with their front legs and heads, similar to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumo_wrestling" title="Sumo wrestling">
       sumo wrestling
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orit_79-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-orit-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Torn flesh around the mouth and teeth, as well as punctures in the rump, can sometimes be observed, although these can also be inflicted during breeding fights.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orit_79-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-orit-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Digestion is very fast in dasyurids and, for the Tasmanian devil, the few hours taken for food to pass through the small gut is a long period in comparison to some other dasyuridae.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t147_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-t147-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devils are known to return to the same places to defecate, and to do so at a communal location, called a
      <i>
       devil latrine
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o25_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o25-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is believed that the communal defecation may be a means of communication that is not well understood.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o25_89-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o25-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devil scats are very large compared to body size; they are on average 15 centimetres (5.9in) long, but there have been samples that are 25 centimetres (9.8in) in length.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o25_89-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o25-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are characteristically grey in colour due to digested bones, or have bone fragments included.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Owen and Pemberton believe that the relationship between Tasmanian devils and thylacines was "close and complex", as they competed directly for prey and probably also for shelter. The thylacines preyed on the devils, the devils scavenged from the thylacine's kills, and the devils ate thylacine young. Menna Jones hypothesises that the two species shared the role of
      <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">
       apex predator
      </a>
      in Tasmania.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Wedge-tailed_eagle" title="Wedge-tailed eagle">
       Wedge-tailed eagles
      </a>
      have a similar carrion-based diet to the devils and are regarded as competitors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Quolls and devils are also seen as being in direct competition in Tasmania. Jones believed that the quoll has evolved into its current state in just 100200 generations of around two years as determined by the equal spacing effect on the devil, the largest species, the spotted-tail quoll, and the smallest species, the eastern quoll.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">
       Reproduction
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanina_Devil_development.PNG">
        <img alt="Black and white diagram indicating various points in a devil's development. Fur is absent until its development from days 50 to 85. The mouth begins round and the lips form from around days 20 to 25. The lips open from around days 80 to 85. They become unattached from their mother at around 100 to 105 days. The east are absent at birth and develop from around days 15 to 18. They are applied to the header until becoming erect from around 72 to 77 days. The eye slits develop around 20 days, and eyelashes between 50 and 55 days. The eyes open between 90 and 100 days." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="717" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Tasmanina_Devil_development.PNG/500px-Tasmanina_Devil_development.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Tasmanina_Devil_development.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Tasmanina_Devil_development.PNG 2x" width="500"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanina_Devil_development.PNG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Developmental steps in the maturation of Tasmanian devil young. The diagonal lines indicate the amount of time the changes take; for example, it takes about 90 days for a devil to develop fur over all its body.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Females start to breed when they reach sexual maturity, typically in their second year. At this point, they become
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estrus_cycle" title="Estrus cycle">
       fertile
      </a>
      once a year, producing multiple
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">
       ova
      </a>
      while in heat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As prey is most abundant in spring and early summer, the devil's reproductive cycle starts in March or April so that the end of the weaning period coincides with the maximisation of food supplies in the wild for the newly roaming young devils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t152_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-t152-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Occurring in March, mating takes places in sheltered locations during both day and night. Males fight over females in the breeding season, and female devils will
      <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">
       mate
      </a>
      with the dominant male.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Females can ovulate up to three times in a 21-day period, and copulation can take five days; one instance of a couple being in the mating den for eight days has been recorded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devils are not
      <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">
       monogamous
      </a>
      , and females will mate with several males if not guarded after mating; males also reproduce with several females during a season.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Females have been shown to be selective in an attempt to ensure the best genetic offspring,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      for example, fighting off the advances of smaller males.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_35-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-draft-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Males often keep their mates in custody in the den, or take them along if they need to drink, lest they engage in infidelity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Males can produce up to 16 offspring over their lifetime, while females average four mating seasons and 12 offspring.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Theoretically this means that a devil population can double on an annual basis and make the species insulated against high mortality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o66_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o66-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The pregnancy rate is high; 80% of two-year-old females were observed with newborns in their pouches during the mating season.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      More recent studies of breeding place the mating season between February and June, as opposed to between February and March.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">
       Gestation
      </a>
      lasts 21 days, and devils give birth to 2030 young standing up,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      each weighing approximately 0.180.24 grams (0.00630.0085oz).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer2001_57-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fischer2001-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At birth, the front limb has well-developed digits with claws; unlike many marsupials, the claws of baby devils are not
      <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">
       deciduous
      </a>
      . As with most other marsupials, the forelimb is longer (0.260.43cm or 0.100.17in) than the rear limb (0.200.28cm or 0.0790.110in), the eyes are spots, and the body is pink. There are no external ears or openings. Unusually, the gender can be determined at birth, with an external scrotum present.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tasmanian devil young are variously called "pups",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "joeys",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or "imps".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When the young are born, competition is fierce as they move from the vagina in a sticky flow of mucus to the pouch. Once inside the pouch, they each remain attached to a nipple for the next 100 days. The female Tasmanian devil's pouch, like that of the
      <a href="/wiki/Wombat" title="Wombat">
       wombat
      </a>
      , opens to the rear, so it is physically difficult for the female to interact with young inside the pouch. Despite the large litter at birth, the female has only four nipples, so there are never more than four babies nursing in the pouch, and the older a female devil gets, the smaller her litters will become. Once the young have made contact with the nipple, it expands, resulting in the oversized nipple being firmly clamped inside the newborn and ensuring that the newborn does not fall out of the pouch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-omate_95-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-omate-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On average, more females survive than males,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and up to 60% of pups do not survive to maturity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o69_59-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-o69-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Inside the pouch, the nourished young develop quickly. In the second week, the
      <a href="/wiki/Rhinarium" title="Rhinarium">
       rhinarium
      </a>
      becomes distinctive and heavily pigmented.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At 15 days, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinna_(anatomy)" title="Pinna (anatomy)">
       external parts of the ear
      </a>
      are visible, although these are attached to the head and do not open out until the devil is around 10 weeks old. The ear begins blackening after around 40 days, when it is less than 1cm (0.39in) long, and by the time the ear becomes erect, it is between 1.2 and 1.6cm (0.47 and 0.63in). Eyelids are apparent at 16 days, whiskers at 17 days, and the lips at 20 days.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The devils can make squeaking noises after eight weeks, and after around 1011 weeks, the lips can open.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite the formation of eyelids, they do not open for three months, although eyelashes form at around 50 days.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The youngup to this point they are pinkstart to grow fur at 49 days and have a full coat by 90 days. The fur growing process starts at the snout and proceeds back through the body, although the tail attains fur before the rump, which is the last part of the body to become covered. Just before the start of the furring process, the colour of the bare devil's skin will darken and become black or dark grey in the tail.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The devils have a complete set of facial
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrissae" title="Vibrissae">
       vibrissae
      </a>
      and ulnar carpels, although it is devoid of
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anconeal" title="wikt:anconeal">
       anconeal
      </a>
      vibrissae. During the third week, the
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mystacial" title="wikt:mystacial">
       mystacials
      </a>
      and ulnarcarpals are the first to form. Subsequently, the
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/infraorbital" title="wikt:infraorbital">
       infraorbital
      </a>
      , interramal,
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/supraorbital" title="wikt:supraorbital">
       supraorbital
      </a>
      and submental vibrissae form. The last four typically occur between the 26th and 39th day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_tasmanian_devils.jpg">
        <img alt="A view from directly above three devils lying with bodies almost touching, on dry leaves, dirt and rocks, under bright sunshine." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Young_tasmanian_devils.jpg/220px-Young_tasmanian_devils.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Young_tasmanian_devils.jpg/330px-Young_tasmanian_devils.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Young_tasmanian_devils.jpg/440px-Young_tasmanian_devils.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_tasmanian_devils.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Three young devils sunbathing
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Their eyes open shortly after their fur coat developsbetween 87 and 93 daysand their mouths can relax their hold of the nipple at 100 days.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They leave the pouch 105 days after birth, appearing as small copies of the parent and weighing around 200 grams (7.1oz).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Zoologist Eric Guiler recorded its size at this time as follows: a
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Crown-snout_length&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Crown-snout length (page does not exist)">
       crown-snout length
      </a>
      of 5.87cm (2.31in), tail length of 5.78cm (2.28in),
      <a href="/wiki/Pes_(anatomy)" title="Pes (anatomy)">
       pes
      </a>
      length 2.94cm (1.16in),
      <a href="/wiki/Manus_(anatomy)" title="Manus (anatomy)">
       manus
      </a>
      2.30cm (0.91in), shank 4.16cm (1.64in), forearm 4.34cm (1.71in) and
      <a href="/wiki/Crown-rump_length" title="Crown-rump length">
       crown-rump length
      </a>
      is 11.9cm (4.7in).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During this period, the devils lengthen at a roughly linear rate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After being ejected, the devils stay outside the pouch, but they remain in the den for around another three months, first venturing outside the den between October and December before becoming independent in January. During this transitional phase out of the pouch, the young devils are relatively safe from predation as they are generally accompanied. When the mother is hunting they can stay inside a shelter or come along, often riding on their mother's back. During this time they continue to drink their mother's milk. Female devils are occupied with raising their young for all but approximately six weeks of the year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The milk contains a higher amount of iron than the milk of placental mammals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_35-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-draft-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Guiler's 1970 study, no females died while rearing their offspring in the pouch. After leaving the pouch, the devils grow by around 0.5kg (1.1lb) a month until they are six months old.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While most pups will survive to be weaned,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Guiler reported that up to three fifths of devils do not reach maturity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o69_59-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-o69-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As juveniles are more crepuscular than adults, their appearance in the open during summer gives the impression to humans of a population boom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o69_59-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-o69-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_diapause" title="Embryonic diapause">
       Embryonic diapause
      </a>
      does not occur.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1970_93-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1970-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Guiler has reported that
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Consecutive_hermaphroditism&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Consecutive hermaphroditism (page does not exist)">
       consecutive hermaphroditism
      </a>
      (sex change) has occurred in captured devils, while Pemberton and Mooney recorded in 2004 the case of an animal with a scrotum and a non-functional pouch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o54_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o54-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In an apparent response to reduced
      <a href="/wiki/Intraspecific_competition" title="Intraspecific competition">
       competition
      </a>
      caused by
      <a href="/wiki/Devil_facial_tumour_disease" title="Devil facial tumour disease">
       devil facial tumour disease
      </a>
      , female devils in regions with the disease are now more likely to begin breeding at one year old.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2008_41-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jones2008-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The disease has also led to the reproductive season being less well-defined, with births more spread out throughout the year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o66_96-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o66-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Litters born to mothers with DFTD have more female pups than male pups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_35-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-draft-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A slow-release
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormonal_contraceptive" title="Hormonal contraceptive">
       hormonal contraceptive
      </a>
      implant for female devils is being developed and tested in a joint program between the Save the Tasmanian Devil program, the
      <a href="/wiki/Zoo_and_Aquarium_Association" title="Zoo and Aquarium Association">
       Zoo and Aquarium Association
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Taronga_Conservation_Society" title="Taronga Conservation Society">
       Taronga Conservation Society
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sydney" title="University of Sydney">
       University of Sydney
      </a>
      . This
      <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_contraceptive" title="Wildlife contraceptive">
       wildlife contraceptive
      </a>
      program is aimed to help the devils continue with their wild behaviour by mating freely, but without certain females contributing too much to the next generation, which "can have long-term genetic consequences for the insurance population". Contraceptive trials in male devils showed that their
      <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">
       testosterone
      </a>
      increased, instead of decreasing as other male mammals' testosterone does. Early studies suggest that the female contraception has been successful, and the female contraceptive implants will be tested in the Maria Island insurance population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_status">
       Conservation status
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Didelphis_cynocephala_and_Didelphis_ursina,_1808.jpg">
        <img alt="A black and white drawing of a devil, which is in the upper half of the picture, facing right, and a thylacine in the lower half, facing left. Both are shown in profile and depicted on a matting of grass or other vegetation." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="711" data-file-width="520" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Didelphis_cynocephala_and_Didelphis_ursina%2C_1808.jpg/220px-Didelphis_cynocephala_and_Didelphis_ursina%2C_1808.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Didelphis_cynocephala_and_Didelphis_ursina%2C_1808.jpg/330px-Didelphis_cynocephala_and_Didelphis_ursina%2C_1808.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Didelphis_cynocephala_and_Didelphis_ursina%2C_1808.jpg/440px-Didelphis_cynocephala_and_Didelphis_ursina%2C_1808.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Didelphis_cynocephala_and_Didelphis_ursina,_1808.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An 1808 impression featuring the Tasmanian devil and a
        <a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">
         Thylacine
        </a>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/George_Prideaux_Robert_Harris" title="George Prideaux Robert Harris">
         George Harris
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Widespread across Australia in the
      <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">
       Pleistocene
      </a>
      , the Tasmanian devil had declined and become restricted to three relict populations during the mid-
      <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">
       Holocene
      </a>
      period around 3,000 years ago. Rock art and a single fossil near Darwin point to a northern population, and remains in the southeast signify a southeastern population ranging from the mouth of the Murray River eastwards to the vicinity of
      <a href="/wiki/Port_Phillip" title="Port Phillip">
       Port Phillip
      </a>
      in Victoria. This population had contracted from northern Victoria and New South Wales. The rising sea levels in the Holocene also cut it off from Tasmanian populations. The third population was from southwest Western Australia. Fossil evidence from this last location has proven controversial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2006_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown2006-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As with many native animals, ancient devils were larger than their contemporary descendants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1972,
      <a href="/wiki/Mike_Archer_(paleontologist)" title="Mike Archer (paleontologist)">
       Mike Archer
      </a>
      and Alex Baynes found a devil tooth at the foot of a cliff near
      <a href="/wiki/Augusta,_Western_Australia" title="Augusta, Western Australia">
       Augusta
      </a>
      in Western Australia and dated it to 430160 years of age, a figure widely circulated and cited.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Australian archaeologist Oliver Brown has disputed this, stating that the authors' uncertainty about the origins of the tooth casts doubts on its age, especially as other remains all date to around 3,000 years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2006_6-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown2006-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The cause of their disappearance from the mainland is unclear, but their decline seems to coincide with the expansion across the mainland of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australian" title="Indigenous Australian">
       indigenous Australians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Dingo" title="Dingo">
       dingoes
      </a>
      . However, whether it was direct hunting by people, competition with dingoes, changes brought about by the increasing human population, who by 3000 years ago were using all habitat types across the continent, or a combination of all three, is unknown; devils had coexisted with dingoes on the mainland for around 3000 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson2003_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Johnson2003-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Brown has also proposed that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o-Southern_Oscillation" title="El Nio-Southern Oscillation">
       El Nio-Southern Oscillation
      </a>
      (ENSO) grew stronger during the Holocene, and that the devil, as a scavenger with a short life span, was highly sensitive to this.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2006_6-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown2006-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In dingo-free Tasmania,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o40_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o40-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      carnivorous marsupials were still active when Europeans arrived. The extermination of the
      <a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">
       thylacine
      </a>
      after the arrival of the Europeans is well known,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but the Tasmanian devil was threatened as well.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PWS_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PWS-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thylacines preyed on devils, and devils attacked thylacine young; devils may have hastened the thylacine's extinction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o44_53-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-o44-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the thylacine was extant, apart from hunting devils, it may also have put pressure on the devil for survival, by competing for scarce food and dens; both animals sought caves and burrows.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o44_53-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-o44-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been speculated that devils may have become more predacious and presided over larger home ranges to fill in the vacancy left by the thylacine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o129_58-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-o129-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Habitat disruption can expose dens where mothers raise their young. This increases mortality, as the mother leaves the disturbed den with her pups clinging to her back, making them more vulnerable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Cancer in general is a common cause of death in devils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2008, high levels of potentially
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcinogenic" title="Carcinogenic">
       carcinogenic
      </a>
      flame retardant chemicals were found in Tasmanian devils. Preliminary results of tests ordered by the Tasmanian government on chemicals found in fat tissue from 16 devils have revealed high levels of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexabromobiphenyl" title="Hexabromobiphenyl">
       hexabromobiphenyl
      </a>
      (BB153) and "reasonably high" levels of
      <a href="/wiki/Decabromodiphenyl_ether" title="Decabromodiphenyl ether">
       decabromodiphenyl ether
      </a>
      (BDE209).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since 1999, all devils caught in the field have had ear
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biopsies" title="Biopsies">
       biopsies
      </a>
      taken, providing samples of DNA. As of September 2010, there are 5,642 samples in this collection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STTDNSept10_77-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-STTDNSept10-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Population_declines">
       Population declines
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      At least two major population declines, possibly due to disease epidemics, have occurred in recorded history: in 1909 and 1950.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guiler1983_37-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guiler1983-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The devil was also reported as scarce in the 1850s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is difficult to estimate the size of the devil population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distimpactdftd_111-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-distimpactdftd-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the mid-1990s, the population was estimated at 130,000150,000 animals,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but this is likely to have been an overestimate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distimpactdftd_111-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-distimpactdftd-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Tasmanian devil's population has been calculated in 2008 by Tasmania's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Primary_Industries_and_Water" title="Department of Primary Industries and Water">
       Department of Primary Industries and Water
      </a>
      as being in the range of 10,000 to 100,000 individuals, with 20,000 to 50,000 mature individuals being likely.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Experts estimate that the devil has suffered a more than 80% decline in its population since the mid-1990s and that only around 10,00015,000 remain in the wild as of 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The species was listed as vulnerable under the Tasmanian
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Threatened_Species_Protection_Act_1995" title="Threatened Species Protection Act 1995">
        Threatened Species Protection Act 1995
       </a>
      </i>
      in 2005
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the Australian
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Environment_Protection_and_Biodiversity_Conservation_Act_1999" title="Environment Protection and Biodiversity Conservation Act 1999">
        Environment Protection and Biodiversity Conservation Act 1999
       </a>
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in 2006, which means that it is at risk of extinction in the "medium term".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vul_56-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-vul-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN">
       IUCN
      </a>
      classified the Tasmanian devil in the lower risk/least concern category in 1996, but in 2009 they reclassified it as endangered.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucntd_1-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-iucntd-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Culling">
       Culling
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The first Tasmanian settlers ate Tasmanian devil, which they described as tasting like veal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Harris-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As it was believed devils would hunt and kill livestock, possibly due to strong imagery of packs of devils eating weak sheep, a bounty scheme to remove the devil from rural properties was introduced as early as 1830.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o9_115-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o9-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, Guiler's research contended that the real cause of livestock losses was poor land management policies and feral dogs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o9_115-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o9-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In areas where the devil is now absent, poultry has continued to be killed by
      <a href="/wiki/Quoll" title="Quoll">
       quolls
      </a>
      . In earlier times, hunting possums and wallabies for fur was a big businessmore than 900,000 animals were hunted in 1923and this resulted in a continuation of bounty hunting of devils as they were thought to be a major threat to the fur industry, even though quolls were more adept at hunting the animals in question.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Over the next 100 years, trapping and poisoning
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      brought them to the brink of extinction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PWS_106-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-PWS-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After the death of the last thylacine in 1936,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the Tasmanian devil was protected by law in June 1941 and the population slowly recovered.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PWS_106-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-PWS-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 1950s, with reports of increasing numbers, some permits to capture devils were granted after complaints of livestock damage. In 1966, poisoning permits were issued although attempts to have the animal unprotected failed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o99_119-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o99-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During this time environmentalists also became more outspoken, particularly as scientific studies provided new data suggesting the threat of devils to livestock had been vastly exaggerated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Numbers may have peaked in the early 1970s after a population boom; in 1975 they were reported to be lighter, possibly due to overpopulation and consequent lack of food.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o118119_121-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o118119-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another report of overpopulation and livestock damage was reported in 1987.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The following year,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Trichinella_spiralis" title="Trichinella spiralis">
        Trichinella spiralis
       </a>
      </i>
      , a parasite which kills animals and can infect humans, was found in devils and minor panic broke out before scientists assured the public that 30% of devils had it but that they could not transmit it to other species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Control permits were ended in the 1990s, but illegal killing continues to a limited extent, albeit "locally intense". This is not considered a substantial problem for the survival of the devil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vul_56-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-vul-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Approximately 10,000 devils were killed per year in the mid-1990s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_35-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-draft-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Foxes">
       Foxes
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feral_foxes_in_Australia" title="Feral foxes in Australia">
       Feral foxes in Australia
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The decline in devil numbers is also seen as an ecological problem, since its presence in the Tasmanian forest
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">
       ecosystem
      </a>
      is believed to have prevented the establishment of the
      <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">
       red fox
      </a>
      , illegally introduced to Tasmania around the turn of the 21st century,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vul_56-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-vul-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bostanci2005_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bostanci2005-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and has limited the number of foxes,
      <a href="/wiki/Feral_cat" title="Feral cat">
       feral cats
      </a>
      and dogs because devils eat carrion that would otherwise feed these creatures,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as killing adult foxes and young foxes in dens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vul_56-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-vul-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Foxes are a problematic
      <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">
       invasive species
      </a>
      in all other
      <a href="/wiki/States_and_territories_of_Australia" title="States and territories of Australia">
       Australian states
      </a>
      and, if established in Tasmania, they would both hinder the recovery of the Tasmanian devil and prey on many other vertebrate species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is believed that Tasmanian devil young would be vulnerable to red fox predation
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and that devils and foxes would directly compete for habitat and dens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One common means of trying to stop the foxes is to use meat baits laced with
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoroacetate" title="Sodium fluoroacetate">
       sodium fluoroacetate
      </a>
      . As devils and other native animals can also be attracted to the poisoned meat, which is placed inside an
      <a href="/wiki/M44_(cyanide_device)" title="M44 (cyanide device)">
       M-44 ejector device
      </a>
      , adaptations have been made to the geometry of the device so that the force and jaw geometry needed to open it fits those of the fox but not the native species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Foxes are not yet sufficiently established in Tasmania to pose a threat to the Tasmanian devil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucntd_1-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-iucntd-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Road_mortality">
       Road mortality
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_roadsign.jpg">
        <img alt="A square-shaped metal tilted at 45 degrees on a metal post. The sign is painted yellow with a picture of a black devil in profile. It is at the side of a straight road cutting through wooded forest and two vehicles can be seen." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Tasmanian_Devil_roadsign.jpg/220px-Tasmanian_Devil_roadsign.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Tasmanian_Devil_roadsign.jpg/330px-Tasmanian_Devil_roadsign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Tasmanian_Devil_roadsign.jpg/440px-Tasmanian_Devil_roadsign.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_roadsign.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A road sign telling drivers that there may be devils nearby
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Motor vehicles are a threat to localised populations of non-abundant Tasmanian mammals,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-r1-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r2_128-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-r2-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and a 2010 study showed that devils were particularly vulnerable. A study of nine species, mostly marsupials of a similar size, showed that devils were more difficult for drivers to detect and thus avoid. At high beam, devils had the lowest detection distance, 40% closer than the median. This requires a 20% reduction in speed for a motorist to avoid the devil. For low beam, the devils are the seventh worst in terms of detection distance, 16% below the median. For avoidance of roadkill to be feasible, motorists would have to drive at around half the current speed limit in rural areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_127-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-r1-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A study in the 1990s on a localised population of devils in a national park in Tasmania recorded a halving of the population after a hitherto gravel access road was upgraded, surfaced with bitumen and widened. At the same time, there was a large increase in deaths caused by vehicles along the new road; there had been none in the preceding six months.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r2_128-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-r2-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The vast majority of deaths occurred in the sealed portion of the road, believed to be due to an increase in speeds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r2_128-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-r2-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was also conjectured that the animals were harder to see against the dark bitumen instead of the light gravel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r2_128-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-r2-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The devil and quoll are especially vulnerable as they often try to retrieve roadkill for food and travel along the road. To alleviate the problem, traffic slowing measures, man-made pathways that offer alternative routes for devils, education campaigns, and the installation of light reflectors to indicate oncoming vehicles have been implemented. They are credited with decreases in roadkill.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r2_128-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-r2-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devils have often been victims of roadkill when they are retrieving other roadkill. Work by scientist Menna Jones and a group of conservation volunteers to remove dead animals from the road resulted in a significant reduction in devil traffic deaths.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o14_83-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o14-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was estimated that 3,392 devils, or between 3.8 and 5.7% of the population, were being killed annually by vehicles in 200104.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vul_56-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-vul-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Devil_facial_tumour_disease">
       Devil facial tumour disease
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Devil_facial_tumour_disease" title="Devil facial tumour disease">
       Devil facial tumour disease
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_Facial_Tumour_Disease.png">
        <img alt="A black devil with several pink-red coloured tumours growing on many parts of its body. The two largest are one covering where its right eye is, and another below the left eye. The right eye is no longer visible and both of these are around one-third the size of a normal devil face. It is lying on a green fabric." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="1011" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Tasmanian_Devil_Facial_Tumour_Disease.png/220px-Tasmanian_Devil_Facial_Tumour_Disease.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Tasmanian_Devil_Facial_Tumour_Disease.png/330px-Tasmanian_Devil_Facial_Tumour_Disease.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Tasmanian_Devil_Facial_Tumour_Disease.png/440px-Tasmanian_Devil_Facial_Tumour_Disease.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tasmanian_Devil_Facial_Tumour_Disease.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Devil facial tumour disease causes tumours to form in and around the mouth, interfering with feeding and eventually leading to death by starvation
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      First seen in 1996 in Mount William in northeastern Tasmania,
      <a href="/wiki/Devil_facial_tumour_disease" title="Devil facial tumour disease">
       devil facial tumour disease
      </a>
      (DFTD) has ravaged Tasmania's wild devils, and estimates of the impact range from 20% to as much as a 50% decline in the devil population, with over 65% of the state affected. The state's west coast area and far north-west are the only places where devils are tumour free.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFTDUpdateJune20005_130-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DFTDUpdateJune20005-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEDMS2005_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEDMS2005-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Individual devils die within months of infection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb3_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb3-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The disease is an example of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmissible_cancer" title="Transmissible cancer">
       transmissible cancer
      </a>
      , which means that it is contagious and passed from one animal to another.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shea_133-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-shea-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Short of a cure, scientists are removing the sick animals and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarantining" title="Quarantining">
       quarantining
      </a>
      healthy devils in case the wild population dies out.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shea_133-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-shea-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because Tasmanian devils have extremely low levels of genetic diversity and a chromosomal mutation unique among carnivorous mammals, they are more prone to the infectious cancer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Wild Tasmanian devil populations are being monitored to track the spread of the disease and to identify changes in disease prevalence. Field monitoring involves trapping devils within a defined area to check for the presence of the disease and determine the number of affected animals. The same area is visited repeatedly to characterise the spread of the disease over time. So far, it has been established that the short-term effects of the disease in an area can be severe. Long-term monitoring at replicated sites will be essential to assess whether these effects remain, or whether populations can recover.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEDMS2005_131-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEDMS2005-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Field workers are also testing the effectiveness of disease suppression by trapping and removing diseased devils. It is hoped that the removal of diseased devils from wild populations should decrease disease prevalence and allow more devils to survive beyond their juvenile years and breed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEDMS2005_131-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEDMS2005-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Research published in the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences">
        Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences
       </a>
      </i>
      on 27 June 2011, suggests picking a genetically diverse breeding stock, defined by the genome sequence, for conservation efforts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2011, it was estimated that it would cost $11 million to preserve the Tasmanian devil species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Research by Professor Greg Woods from the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tasmania" title="University of Tasmania">
       University of Tasmania
      </a>
      's
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Menzies_Institute_for_Medical_Research&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Menzies Institute for Medical Research (page does not exist)">
       Menzies Institute for Medical Research
      </a>
      has shown encouraging evidence for the potential development of a vaccine using dead Devil Facial Tumour Disease cells to trigger an immune response in healthy devils.
