Field testing of the vaccine is being undertaken as a collaborative project between the Menzies Institute for Medical Research and the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Save_the_Tasmanian_Devil_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Save the Tasmanian Devil Program (page does not exist)">
       Save the Tasmanian Devil Program
      </a>
      under the Wild Devil Recovery program, and aims to test the immunization protocol as a tool in ensuring the devil's long term survival in the wild.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">
       Relationship with humans
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      At
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Nitchie&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lake Nitchie (page does not exist)">
       Lake Nitchie
      </a>
      in western
      <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">
       New South Wales
      </a>
      in 1970, a male human skeleton wearing a necklace of 178 teeth from 49 different devils was found. The skeleton is estimated to be 7000 years old, and the necklace is believed to be much older than the skeleton. Archeologist
      <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Flood" title="Josephine Flood">
       Josephine Flood
      </a>
      believes the devil was hunted for its teeth and that this contributed to its extinction on mainland Australia. Owen and Pemberton note that few such necklaces have been found.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Middens that contain devil bones are raretwo notable examples are
      <a href="/wiki/Devil%27s_Lair" title="Devil's Lair">
       Devil's Lair
      </a>
      in the south-western part of Western Australia and
      <a href="/wiki/Tower_Hill_State_Game_Reserve" title="Tower Hill State Game Reserve">
       Tower Hill
      </a>
      in Victoria.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o41_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o41-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Tasmania,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Aboriginals" title="Tasmanian Aboriginals">
       local Aborigines
      </a>
      and devils sheltered in the same caves. Tasmanian Aboriginal names for the devil recorded by Europeans include "tarrabah", "poirinnah", and "par-loo-mer-rer".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to Fritz Noetling, the Secretary of the Royal Society of Tasmania in 1910, there was no evidence that Tasmanian Aborigines ate any carnivorous animals. Owen and Pemberton feel this may have contributed to the devil's survival prior to European settlement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o41_27-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-o41-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Convicts at the time when Hobart was being settled in made a meal of Tasmanian devils and it was claimed to be not unlike veal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is a common belief that devils will eat humans. While they are known to eat the bodies of murder victims or people who have committed suicide, there are prevalent myths that it eats living humans who wander into the bush.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite outdated beliefs and exaggerations regarding their disposition, many, although not all, devils will remain still when in the presence of a human; some will also shake nervously. It can bite and scratch out of fear when held by a human, but a firm grip will cause it to remain still.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although they can be tamed, they are asocial and are not considered appropriate as pets;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o25_89-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-o25-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      they have an unpleasant odour and neither demonstrate nor respond to affection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o113_143-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o113-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Until recently, the devil was not studied much by academics and naturalists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o4_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o4-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the start of the 20th century, Hobart zoo operator Mary Roberts, who was not a trained scientist, was credited for changing people's attitudes and encouraging scientific interest in native animals such as the devil that were seen as fearsome and abhorrent, and the human perception of the animal changed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orob_145-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-orob-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Thomson_Flynn" title="Theodore Thomson Flynn">
       Theodore Thomson Flynn
      </a>
      was the first professor of biology in Tasmania and carried out some research during the period around World War I.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the mid-1960s Professor Guiler assembled a team of researchers and started a decade of systematic fieldwork on the devil. This is seen as the start of modern scientific study of it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the devil was still negatively depicted, including in tourism material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o99_119-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o99-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first doctorate awarded for research into the devil came in 1991.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o4_144-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-o4-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_captivity">
       In captivity
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sarcophilus_harrisii_-Healesville_Sanctuary-8a.jpg">
        <img alt="A devil with red ears and white patches under its neck, is standing on some bark chips, in front of some grass and behind a rock of the size of its body." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1892" data-file-width="2293" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Sarcophilus_harrisii_-Healesville_Sanctuary-8a.jpg/220px-Sarcophilus_harrisii_-Healesville_Sanctuary-8a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Sarcophilus_harrisii_-Healesville_Sanctuary-8a.jpg/330px-Sarcophilus_harrisii_-Healesville_Sanctuary-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Sarcophilus_harrisii_-Healesville_Sanctuary-8a.jpg/440px-Sarcophilus_harrisii_-Healesville_Sanctuary-8a.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sarcophilus_harrisii_-Healesville_Sanctuary-8a.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        At
        <a href="/wiki/Healesville_Sanctuary" title="Healesville Sanctuary">
         Healesville Sanctuary
        </a>
        , Victoria
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Early attempts to breed Tasmanian devils in captivity had limited success. The animal activist Mary Roberts, who was known for her work in presenting a less hostile image of the devil to the public and wrote about it in academic journals,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orob_145-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-orob-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      bred a pair at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beaumaris_Zoo" title="Beaumaris Zoo">
       Beaumaris Zoo
      </a>
      which she named Billy and Truganini in 1913. However, although advised to remove Billy, Roberts found Truganini too distressed by his absence, and returned him. The first litter was presumed eaten by Billy, but a second litter in 1914 survived after Billy was removed. Roberts wrote an article on keeping and breeding the devils for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Zoological_Society" title="London Zoological Society">
       London Zoological Society
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orob_145-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-orob-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even by 1934, successful breeding of the devil was rare.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a study on the growth of young devils in captivity, some developmental stages were very different from those reported by Guiler. The pinnae were free on day 36, and eyes opened later, on days 115121.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In general, females tend to retain more stress after being taken into captivity than males.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A plan to create "insurance populations" of disease-free devils has been ongoing since 2005. As of June 2012, the insurance population has reached a combined total of 500 animals and representing over 98% of the genetic diversity of this species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STDP_151-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-STDP-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of these devils are living in Australian zoos and wildlife reserves. Beginning in November 2012 however, in an effort to create a population that is both wild and disease-free, Tasmanian devils have been relocated to
      <a href="/wiki/Maria_Island" title="Maria Island">
       Maria Island
      </a>
      , a mountainous island off the east coast of Tasmania.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STDP_151-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-STDP-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Maria Island population has grown from a starting population of twenty-eight to 90 and experts will soon begin transferring healthy devils back to the mainland population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tasmanian devils were displayed in various zoos around the world from the 1850s onwards.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o132_153-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o132-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 1950s several animals were given to European zoos.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In October 2005 the Tasmanian government sent four devils, two male and two female, to the
      <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Zoo" title="Copenhagen Zoo">
       Copenhagen Zoo
      </a>
      , following the birth of the
      <a href="/wiki/Prince_Christian_of_Denmark" title="Prince Christian of Denmark">
       first son
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Frederik,_Crown_Prince_of_Denmark" title="Frederik, Crown Prince of Denmark">
       Frederik, Crown Prince of Denmark
      </a>
      and his Tasmanian-born wife
      <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Crown_Princess_of_Denmark" title="Mary, Crown Princess of Denmark">
       Mary
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to restrictions on their export by the Australian government, at the time these were the only devils known to be living outside Australia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fed_34-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-fed-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 2013, due to the successes of the insurance population program, it was planned to send devils to other zoos around the world in a pilot program.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
       <a href="#cite_note-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Zoo_Global" title="San Diego Zoo Global">
       San Diego Zoo Global
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque_Biological_Park" title="Albuquerque Biological Park">
       Albuquerque Biopark
      </a>
      were selected to participate in the program,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
       <a href="#cite_note-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Wellington_Zoo" title="Wellington Zoo">
       Wellington Zoo
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Auckland_Zoo" title="Auckland Zoo">
       Auckland Zoo
      </a>
      soon followed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
       <a href="#cite_note-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Captive devils are usually forced to stay awake during the day to cater to visitors, rather than following their natural nocturnal style.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-o133_159-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-o133-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There have been reports and suspicions of illegal trading in the past. In 1997, a devil turned up in Western Australia; it had not escaped from any licensed keeper. During the 1990s there were internet sites in the US that were advertising devil sales, and rumours that some US Navy personnel had tried to buy them illegally during a visit to Tasmania.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
       <a href="#cite_note-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_references">
       Cultural references
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taz_Looney_Tunes_parade.jpg">
        <img alt="A black open-top sports car with lights on is being driven down an asphalt road. A large furry toy costume, slightly larger than a human is standing in the back seat. It has cream coloured mouth and chest, and dark brown arms and forehead, large whiskers, a grin, large white eyes and two canines. Behind him are some men walking in green costumes. On the left is a crowd watching the parade from the footpath, in front of tall buildings with stone arches." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Taz_Looney_Tunes_parade.jpg/220px-Taz_Looney_Tunes_parade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Taz_Looney_Tunes_parade.jpg/330px-Taz_Looney_Tunes_parade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Taz_Looney_Tunes_parade.jpg/440px-Taz_Looney_Tunes_parade.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taz_Looney_Tunes_parade.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Warner Bros' Tasmanian Devil at a parade in California
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The devil is an iconic animal within Australia, particularly Tasmania; it is the symbol of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tasmanian_National_Parks_and_Wildlife_Service" title="Tasmanian National Parks and Wildlife Service">
       Tasmanian National Parks and Wildlife Service
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPIWEweb1_44-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-DPIWEweb1-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the former Tasmanian Australian rules football team which played in the
      <a href="/wiki/Victorian_Football_League" title="Victorian Football League">
       Victorian Football League
      </a>
      was known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Devils_Football_Club" title="Tasmanian Devils Football Club">
       Devils
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Hobart_Devils" title="Hobart Devils">
       Hobart Devils
      </a>
      were once part of the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_League_(Australia)" title="National Basketball League (Australia)">
       National Basketball League
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
       <a href="#cite_note-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The devil has appeared on several
      <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_Australian_dollar#Collectable_coins" title="Coins of the Australian dollar">
       commemorative coins
      </a>
      in Australia over the years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
       <a href="#cite_note-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
       <a href="#cite_note-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Cascade_Brewery" title="Cascade Brewery">
       Cascade Brewery
      </a>
      in Tasmania sells a
      <a href="/wiki/Ginger_beer" title="Ginger beer">
       ginger beer
      </a>
      with a Tasmanian devil on the label.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
       <a href="#cite_note-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2015, the Tasmanian Devil was chosen as Tasmania's
      <a href="/wiki/Symbols_of_Tasmania" title="Symbols of Tasmania">
       state emblem
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tasmanian devils are popular with tourists, and the director of the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tasmanian_Devil_Conservation_Park&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tasmanian Devil Conservation Park (page does not exist)">
       Tasmanian Devil Conservation Park
      </a>
      has described their possible extinction as "a really significant blow for Australian and Tasmanian tourism".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There has also been a multi-million dollar proposal to build a giant 19m-high, 35m-long devil in
      <a href="/wiki/Launceston,_Tasmania" title="Launceston, Tasmania">
       Launceston
      </a>
      in northern Tasmania as a tourist attraction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Devils began to be used in tourism from the 1970s, when studies showed that the animals were often the only things known about Tasmania overseas and suggested that they should therefore be the centrepiece of marketing efforts, resulting in some devils being taken on promotional tours.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      With their unique personality, the Tasmanian devil has been the subject of numerous documentaries, fiction and non-fiction children's books.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parer_170-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-parer-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
       <a href="#cite_note-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
       <a href="#cite_note-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Royalties from
      <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Wild" title="Margaret Wild">
       Margaret Wild
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       Ruby Roars
      </i>
      , about a Tasmanian devil, are going to research into DFTD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2005 Australian documentary on the Tasmanian devil,
      <i>
       Terrors of Tasmania
      </i>
      , directed and produced by
      <a href="/wiki/David_Parer" title="David Parer">
       David Parer
      </a>
      and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Elizabeth_Parer-Cook&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Elizabeth Parer-Cook (page does not exist)">
       Elizabeth Parer-Cook
      </a>
      , follows a female devil called
      <i>
       Manganinnie
      </i>
      through breeding season and the birth and rearing of her young. The documentary also looks at the effect of devil facial tumour disease and the conservation measures being taken to ensure survival of the Tasmanian devil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parer_170-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-parer-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has screened on television in Australia and in the United States on the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Channel" title="National Geographic Channel">
       National Geographic Channel
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parer_170-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-parer-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
       <a href="#cite_note-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Tasmanian devil is probably best known internationally as the inspiration for the
      <i>
       Looney Tunes
      </i>
      cartoon character
      <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Devil_(Looney_Tunes)" title="Tasmanian Devil (Looney Tunes)">
       the Tasmanian Devil
      </a>
      , or "Taz" in 1954. Little known at the time, the loud hyperactive cartoon character has little in common with the real life animal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After a few shorts between 1957 and 1964, the character was retired until the 1990s, when he gained his own show,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Taz-Mania" title="Taz-Mania">
        Taz-Mania
       </a>
      </i>
      , and again became popular.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1997, a newspaper report noted that
      <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">
       Warner Bros.
      </a>
      had "trademarked the character and registered the name Tasmanian Devil", and that this trademark "was policed", including an eight-year legal case to allow a Tasmanian company to call a fishing lure "Tasmanian Devil". Debate followed, and a delegation from the Tasmanian government met with Warner Bros.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
       <a href="#cite_note-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Ray_Groom" title="Ray Groom">
       Ray Groom
      </a>
      , the Tourism Minister, later announced that a "verbal agreement" had been reached. An annual fee would be paid to Warner Bros. in return for the Government of Tasmania being able to use the image of Taz for "marketing purposes". This agreement later disappeared.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2006, Warner Bros. permitted the Government of Tasmania to sell stuffed toys of Taz with profits funnelled into research on DFTD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
       <a href="#cite_note-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There is a
      <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">
       DC Comics
      </a>
      superhero called
      <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Devil_(comics)" title="Tasmanian Devil (comics)">
       Tasmanian Devil
      </a>
      who is a member of the
      <a href="/wiki/Global_Guardians" title="Global Guardians">
       Global Guardians
      </a>
      team.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dc-glob_182-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dc-glob-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Snarl_(Transformers)#Beast_Wars" title="Snarl (Transformers)">
       Snarl
      </a>
      , a character in the
      <a href="/wiki/Transformers_(comics)" title="Transformers (comics)">
       Transformers
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Beast_Wars_(IDW_Publishing)" title="Beast Wars (IDW Publishing)">
        Beast Wars
       </a>
      </i>
      storyline, had the alternate form of a Tasmanian devil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Kid" title="Tasmanian Kid">
       Tasmanian Kid
      </a>
      from
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beast_Wars_II" title="Beast Wars II">
        Beast Wars II
       </a>
      </i>
      could also transform into a Tasmanian devil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
       <a href="#cite_note-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Researchers have named a genetic-mutant mouse "the Tasmanian Devil". It is defective in the development of sensory-hair cells of the ear, leading it to abnormal behaviours including head-tossing and circling,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      more like the cartoon "Taz" than the actual Tasmanian Devil.
     </p>
     <p>
      For the 2.6.29 release of the
      <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">
       Linux
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
       kernel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">
       Linus Torvalds
      </a>
      temporarily replaced the
      <a href="/wiki/Tux" title="Tux">
       Tux
      </a>
      mascot with a Tasmanian devil named
      <i>
       Tuz
      </i>
      , in support of the Save the Tasmanian Devil Campaign.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
